17/02/25, 22:36 - Parth IITB Chem: 1.     Student A 3D printed a part and deformed it to size. Another student B cast the part and thereafter deformed it. Which of the two is likely to get a better dimensional accuracy on the part assuming material for manufacture to be the same?

Question 10Answer

a.
Student A will get better accuracy


b.
Student B will get better accuracy


c.
Can‚Äôt say


d.
Both A and B will get similar level of accuracy
ChatGPT said:
The correct answer is a. Student A will get better accuracy.

Explanation:
3D printing generally provides better dimensional accuracy because the part is built layer by layer under controlled conditions. Any deformation needed can be more precise as the initial dimensions are closer to the final desired size.

In contrast, casting involves cooling and solidification, which can cause shrinkage and warping due to uneven cooling rates and internal stresses. Deforming the cast part afterward may introduce additional inaccuracies.

You said:
   Identify the correct statement(s)

Question 12Answer

a.
All surfaces MUST be finished by grinding


b.
All features MUST be dimensioned


c.
All dimensions MUST have tolerances assigned to each


d.
All features need not be dimensioned


e.
Dimensions should have a common reference
ChatGPT said:
The correct answers are:

d. All features need not be dimensioned
e. Dimensions should have a common reference
Explanation:
(d) Not all features need to be dimensioned if they are implied by other dimensions or are non-critical.
(e) Using a common reference (datum) ensures consistency and accuracy in measurements and manufacturing.
Why not the others?
(a) Not all surfaces require grinding; different manufacturing processes and functional requirements dictate the needed surface finish.
(b) Not every feature needs individual dimensions if they can be inferred from other measurements.
(c) Not all dimensions require specific tolerances; some may follow general tolerance standards.
You said:
Mark the statements which are true

Question 14Answer

a.
Soldering may be done where joint is required to be easily undone


b.
Soldering does not involve melting of materials in the two pieces to be joined


c.
Solder forms an alloy with the metals to be joined together


d.
Solder reacts with the metal to be joined during soldering


e.
Welding produces a stronger joint than soldering


f.
Welding involves melting of metal in both the pieces to be joined
ChatGPT said:
The correct answers are:

a. Soldering may be done where joint is required to be easily undone
b. Soldering does not involve melting of materials in the two pieces to be joined
c. Solder forms an alloy with the metals to be joined together
e. Welding produces a stronger joint than soldering
f. Welding involves melting of metal in both the pieces to be joined
Explanation:
(a) Soldered joints can be undone by reheating the solder.
(b) In soldering, only the solder melts, not the base materials.
(c) Solder forms a metallurgical bond by creating a thin alloy layer at the interface.
(e) Welding provides a stronger joint because it involves melting and fusing the base metals.
(f) Welding melts the base metals to create a strong, cohesive joint.
Why not (d)?
(d) Solder does not chemically react with the base metals; it forms a physical bond through alloying.
You said:
  The additive manufacturing methods that involve melting are

Question 15Answer

a.
All of the above


b.
Stereolithography (SLA)


c.
Fused Deposition Modelling (FDM)


d.
Selective laser Melting (SLM)


e.
Selective laser Sintering (SLS)
ChatGPT said:
The correct answer is:

d. Selective laser Melting (SLM)
Explanation:
SLM (Selective Laser Melting) involves complete melting of metal powder using a laser to form fully dense parts.
Why not the others?
(b) SLA (Stereolithography) uses a UV laser to cure liquid resin without melting.
(c) FDM (Fused Deposition Modelling) involves heating thermoplastic filament until it's soft enough to extrude but not fully melted.
(e) SLS (Selective Laser Sintering) involves sintering (heating without melting) to fuse powder particles.
You said:
    The main process in machining is

Question 16Answer

a.
Permanent deformation


b.
None of the above


c.
Welding


d.
Casting


e.
Shearing
ChatGPT said:
The correct answer is:

e. Shearing
Explanation:
In machining, material is removed from the workpiece in the form of chips through a shearing action. The cutting tool applies a force that causes the material to shear off along a defined plane, forming chips.
You said:
CO2 lasers are used (in preference to Neodymium lasers) for cutting polymers because

Question 2Answer

a.
Availability of Neodymium Is much more scarce than CO2 which is plentifully available


b.
Neodymium based lasers have a smaller wavelength and hence a greater power


c.
Polymers melt at relatively lower temperatures


d.
CO2 lasers have a higher wavelength and hence lower power


e.
Neodymium based lasers are suitable for engraving on polymers but not for cutting them
ChatGPT said:
The correct answer is:

d. CO‚ÇÇ lasers have a higher wavelength and hence lower power
Explanation:
CO‚ÇÇ lasers have a longer wavelength (usually around 10.6 ¬µm), which is efficiently absorbed by polymers, making them suitable for cutting. Their lower power density compared to Neodymium lasers prevents excessive burning or degradation of polymers.
Neodymium lasers have a shorter wavelength (around 1.06 ¬µm), which is more suitable for cutting metals due to higher power density but is not as effective for polymers.
You said:
For laser rastering, which of the following statements are true

Question 8Answer

a.
Low power gives a dark coloured raster pattern


b.
High laser power gives a dark coloured raster pattern


c.
Laser creates grayscale images by controlling the laser power


d.
Laser evaporates the surface layers of the substrate


e.
None of the above
ChatGPT said:
The correct answers are:

c. Laser creates grayscale images by controlling the laser power
d. Laser evaporates the surface layers of the substrate
Explanation:
(c) Grayscale images are achieved by varying the laser power, which changes the intensity and depth of the raster pattern.
(d) During rastering, the laser evaporates thin layers of the material's surface, creating contrasts and patterns.
Why not the others?
(a) Low power generally produces lighter marks.
(b) High power usually results in deeper, lighter areas due to more material removal, not darker patterns.

\title{\color{MainTitleClr}\bfseries\LARGE Bird Species Identification using CUB-200-2011\\[0.5em]

\section*{Abstract}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Abstract}
This report details the implementation of a fine-grained bird classification system for the CUB-200-2011 dataset. We employ a lightweight EfficientNet-B0 architecture (~5.3M params), bounding-box cropping, and advanced augmentations. The model, trained with AdamW, label smoothing, and a cosine learning rate schedule, reaches a final test accuracy of \textbf{83.07\%} in 43 minutes.

\section*{Introduction}

Fine-grained image classification is a specialized computer vision subfield dedicated to differentiating between very similar sub-categories of a general object class. In contrast to broad object recognition (e.g., "dog" versus "cat"), fine-grained classification targets distinctions like different dog breeds, car models, or, as in this project, specific bird species. This task poses distinct challenges because the visual cues separating sub-categories are often subtle, demanding that models learn minute, detailed features.

The capacity for accurate fine-grained classification holds substantial value in numerous fields. In biological sciences, it assists with biodiversity tracking, species identification, and ecological research. For wildlife conservation, the precise recognition of endangered species is paramount. Applications also span industrial quality control, medical diagnostics, and specialized security systems.

This project focuses on fine-grained bird classification using the \textbf{CUB-200-2011 (Caltech-UCSD Birds 200-2011)} dataset. We selected this dataset as it is a standard benchmark for fine-grained tasks, presenting 200 bird species with high intra-class variance (e.g., varied poses, lighting, and occlusions) and low inter-class variance (e.g., related species with minor plumage differences). These attributes make CUB-200-2011 an excellent platform for assessing the robustness and discrimination of deep learning models in difficult scenarios.

The core objectives of this mini-project are:
\begin{enumerate}
    \item \textbf{To construct an efficient and powerful deep learning pipeline} for fine-grained bird classification with a contemporary convolutional neural network.
    \item \textbf{To obtain competitive classification accuracy} on the CUB-200-2011 test data, proving the model's capability to discern highly similar bird species.
    \item \textbf{To optimize for model size and training duration}, demonstrating a practical fine-grained classification method that balances performance with computational demands.
\end{enumerate}

This document outlines our comprehensive methodology, covering custom data handling, advanced augmentation techniques, model architecture choices, and training procedures, and concludes with an analysis of the final performance.



\section{Dataset and Preprocessing}
\subsection{Dataset Overview}
CUB-200-2011 contains 11,788 images of 200 bird species. We adhere to the standard train-test partition: \textbf{5,994 images for training} and \textbf{5,794 for testing}.

\begin{lstlisting}[caption={CUBDataset Implementation}, label={lst:cubdataset}]
import os
from PIL import Image
import torch
from torch.utils.data import Dataset

class CUBDataset(Dataset):
    def __init__(self, root_dir, split='train', transform=None):
        
        self.root_dir = root_dir
        self.transform = transform

        # 1) Read image file paths
        with open(os.path.join(root_dir, 'images.txt')) as f:
            # {image_id: relative_path_string}
            self.id_to_path = {
                int(line.split()[0]): line.strip().split()[1]
                for line in f
            }

        # 2) Read class labels
        with open(os.path.join(root_dir, 'image_class_labels.txt')) as f:
          self.id_to_label = {
              int(parts[0]): int(parts[1]) - 1
              for parts in (line.strip().split() for line in f)
          }

        # 3) Read train/test split flags
        with open(os.path.join(root_dir, 'train_test_split.txt')) as f:
          self.id_to_split = {
              int(parts[0]): int(parts[1])
              for parts in (line.strip().split() for line in f)
          }

        # 4) Read bounding box coordinates
        with open(os.path.join(root_dir, 'bounding_boxes.txt')) as f:
          self.id_to_bbox = {
              int(parts[0]): tuple(map(float, parts[1:]))
              for parts in (line.strip().split() for line in f)
          }

        # 5) Filter data based on the specified split
        split_flag = 1 if split == 'train' else 0
        filtered_ids = [
            i for i in self.id_to_path
            if self.id_to_split[i] == split_flag
        ]
        self.samples = [
            (i, self.id_to_path[i], self.id_to_label[i], self.id_to_bbox[i])
            for i in filtered_ids
        ]

    def __len__(self):
        return len(self.samples)

    def __getitem__(self, idx):
        img_id, rel_path, label, bbox = self.samples[idx]
        img_path = os.path.join(self.root_dir, 'images', rel_path)
        img = Image.open(img_path).convert('RGB')

        # --- Crop to the bounding box ---
        x, y, w, h = bbox
        left   = int(x)
        top    = int(y)
        right  = int(x + w)
        bottom = int(y + h)
        img = img.crop((left, top, right, bottom))

        # --- Apply specified transformations ---
        if self.transform:
            img = self.transform(img)

        return img, label

\end{lstlisting}
\section{Custom Dataset Class: \textsf{CUBDataset}}

To manage the CUB-200-2011 dataset effectively, we created a custom PyTorch \textsf{Dataset} class, \textsf{CUBDataset}. This class provides organized loading of images, their bounding boxes, and labels, while accommodating transformations for training and evaluation.

\subsection*{Key Features}

\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Initialization (\textsf{\_\_init\_\_})}: Reads metadata from the dataset's root folder, including:
    \begin{itemize}
        \item \textsf{images.txt}: Connects image IDs to their relative file paths.
        \item \textsf{image\_class\_labels.txt}: Connects image IDs to class labels (adjusted to be 0-indexed).
        \item \textsf{train\_test\_split.txt}: Indicates if an image belongs to the training or test set.
        \item \textsf{bounding\_boxes.txt}: Contains bounding box data (x, y, width, height).
    \end{itemize}
    
    \item \textbf{Split Management}: Depending on the `split` parameter ('train' or 'test'), the class filters and retains only the appropriate images and associated metadata.

    \item \textbf{\textsf{\_\_getitem\_\_} Method}:
    \begin{itemize}
        \item Fetches the image using PIL.
        \item Crops the image to its bounding box using the loaded coordinates.
        \item Applies the given transform pipeline (e.g., augmentations, resizing).
        \item Returns the processed image and its class label.
    \end{itemize}

    \item \textbf{Bounding Box Cropping}:
    \begin{itemize}
        \item The bounding box data \textsf{(x, y, width, height)} is translated to a \textsf{(left, top, right, bottom)} crop box.
        \item This step ensures the model trains primarily on the bird (foreground), enhancing focus and minimizing background distraction.
    \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

% --- Bounding Box Cropping Visual Example ---
% You will need to create or find an image that shows an original bird image

% Replace 'path/to/original_and_cropped_example.png' with your actual image path.

Left to right: the initial image (with its ID), the same image with its ground-truth bounding box in red, and the final image cropped to that box. This shows the preprocessing that isolates the bird, aiding the model in focusing on fine-grained details while reducing background interference.}

    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=1\linewidth]{original v cropped.png}
    \caption{Demonstration of Bounding Box Cropping: An original image (left) contrasted with the same image cropped to its bounding box (right). This preprocessing step isolates the bird to reduce background noise and guide the model's focus toward the subject's fine-grained features.}
    \label{fig:bbox_cropping_example}
\end{figure}

\subsection*{Why It Is Important}

Employing a custom dataset class gave us complete control over data handling. This facilitated:
\begin{itemize}
    \item Smooth integration of bounding-box cropping.
    \item Adherence to the official train/test splits.
    \item The flexibility to use different transformations for training versus evaluation.
\end{itemize}


\section{Model Training Pipeline}

The training script, \textsf{train\_efficientnet.py}, orchestrates the entire process of training EfficientNet-B0 on CUB-200-2011. The code is designed to be modular, clear, and optimized for both efficiency and accuracy.
\begin{lstlisting}[caption={EfficientNet-B0 Training Script}, label={lst:efficientnet}]
import os, time
import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import torch.optim as optim
from torch.optim.lr_scheduler import CosineAnnealingWarmRestarts
from torch.utils.data import DataLoader
import torchvision.transforms as T
from torchvision.models import efficientnet_b0, EfficientNet_B0_Weights
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

from data_loader import CUBDataset 

# 1. Setup Configuration
DEVICE      = torch.device("cuda" if torch.cuda.is_available() else "cpu")
DATA_ROOT   = "CUB_200_2011/CUB_200_2011"
BATCH_SIZE  = 64
NUM_EPOCHS  = 40
LR          = 1e-4
WEIGHT_DEC  = 1e-4
NUM_CLASSES = 200
\end{lstlisting}
\subsection*{1. Configuration and Hyperparameters}
First, we define key parameters:
\begin{itemize}
  \item \textsf{DEVICE}: Defaults to GPU if one is detected.
  \item \textsf{BATCH\_SIZE}: 64
  \item \textsf{NUM\_EPOCHS}: 40
  \item \textsf{LR}: 1e-4 (learning rate), \textsf{WEIGHT\_DECAY}: 1e-4
  \item \textsf{NUM\_CLASSES}: 200 (matching the dataset)
\end{itemize}
\begin{lstlisting}[caption={EfficientNet-B0 Training Script}, label={lst:efficientnet}]
train_transform = T.Compose([
    T.Resize((256, 256)),
    T.RandomCrop(224),
    T.RandomHorizontalFlip(),
    T.AutoAugment(policy=T.AutoAugmentPolicy.IMAGENET),
    T.ColorJitter(brightness=0.2, contrast=0.2),
    T.ToTensor(),
    T.RandomErasing(p=0.5),
])
test_transform = T.Compose([
    T.Resize((224, 224)),
    T.ToTensor(),
])
\end{lstlisting}
\subsection{Data Augmentation and Preprocessing}
We apply transformations using \texttt{torchvision.transforms}. Augmentation is vital for deep learning, especially in fine-grained tasks, as it diversifies training data, which enhances model generalization and mitigates overfitting.

\textbf{Training transformations:}
\begin{itemize}
    \item Resize images to $256\times256$, then take a $224\times224$ random crop.
    \item Apply a random horizontal flip.
    \item \textbf{AutoAugment (ImageNet policy)}: This method automatically discovers an effective data augmentation strategy from a set of possible operations, boosting model accuracy and robustness beyond manually designed policies.
    \item Jitter color properties (brightness and contrast).
    \item \textbf{Random Erasing ($p=0.5$)}: This technique randomly masks a rectangular patch in an image. It serves as regularization, forcing the model to be more resilient to occlusions and less dependent on specific features, thereby improving generalization.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Testing transformations:}
\begin{itemize}
  \item Simply resize to $224\times224$ and convert to a tensor.
\end{itemize}
\begin{lstlisting}[caption={EfficientNet-B0 Training Script}, label={lst:efficientnet}]
train_dataset = CUBDataset(DATA_ROOT, split='train', transform=train_transform)
test_dataset  = CUBDataset(DATA_ROOT, split='test',  transform=test_transform)
train_loader = DataLoader(train_dataset, batch_size=BATCH_SIZE, shuffle=True,  num_workers=4)
test_loader  = DataLoader(test_dataset,  batch_size=BATCH_SIZE, shuffle=False, num_workers=4)
\end{lstlisting}
\subsection*{3. Dataset and DataLoader}
We instantiate our custom \textsf{CUBDataset} with bounding box cropping. DataLoaders are set up for training and testing, with shuffling for the training set and multiple workers for efficiency.
\begin{lstlisting}[caption={EfficientNet-B0 Training Script}, label={lst:efficientnet}]
weights = EfficientNet_B0_Weights.IMAGENET1K_V1
model = efficientnet_b0(weights=weights)
model.classifier[1] = nn.Linear(model.classifier[1].in_features, NUM_CLASSES)
model = model.to(DEVICE)

\end{lstlisting}
\subsection*{Model Initialization}
\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Backbone}: An EfficientNet-B0 model from \texttt{torchvision.models} is used, pretrained on ImageNet weights. This transfer learning approach leverages features learned from a massive dataset.
    \item \textbf{Classifier Adaptation}: The original classifier head of the EfficientNet-B0 is substituted with a new fully connected (FC) layer. The input features of this new layer match the backbone's output, and its output size is \texttt{NUM\_CLASSES} (200) to match the bird species. \textit{Initially, the backbone was frozen to train the new head, after which the whole model was fine-tuned.}
    \item \textbf{Device Placement}: The complete model is moved to the designated compute device (CPU/GPU).
\end{itemize}
\begin{lstlisting}[caption={EfficientNet-B0 Training Script}, label={lst:efficientnet}]
criterion = nn.CrossEntropyLoss(label_smoothing=0.1)
optimizer = optim.AdamW(model.parameters(), lr=LR, weight_decay=WEIGHT_DEC)
scheduler = CosineAnnealingWarmRestarts(optimizer, T_0=10, T_mult=2, eta_min=1e-5)
\end{lstlisting}
\subsection*{Loss, Optimizer, and Scheduler}
\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Loss Function}: CrossEntropyLoss with a label smoothing factor of 0.1.
    \item \textbf{Optimizer}: The AdamW optimizer with weight decay.
    \item \textbf{Scheduler}: Cosine Annealing with Warm Restarts to periodically adjust the learning rate.
\end{itemize}
\begin{lstlisting}[caption={EfficientNet-B0 Training Script}, label={lst:efficientnet}]
history = {'train_loss': [], 'test_loss': [], 'train_acc': [], 'test_acc': []}
best_acc = 0.0

start_time = time.time()

for epoch in range(1, NUM_EPOCHS+1):
    # ---- TRAINING PHASE ----
    model.train()
    running_loss, correct_preds, total_samples = 0.0, 0, 0
    for images, targets in train_loader:
        images, targets = images.to(DEVICE), targets.to(DEVICE)
        optimizer.zero_grad()
        outputs = model(images)
        loss = criterion(outputs, targets)
        loss.backward()
        optimizer.step()
        running_loss += loss.item() * images.size(0)
        preds = outputs.argmax(1)
        correct_preds += preds.eq(targets).sum().item()
        total_samples += targets.size(0)
    epoch_train_loss = running_loss / total_samples
    epoch_train_acc = 100. * correct_preds / total_samples

    # ---- VALIDATION PHASE ----
    model.eval()
    running_loss, correct_preds, total_samples = 0.0, 0, 0
    with torch.no_grad():
        for images, targets in test_loader:
            images, targets = images.to(DEVICE), targets.to(DEVICE)
            outputs = model(images)
            loss = criterion(outputs, targets)
            running_loss += loss.item() * images.size(0)
            preds = outputs.argmax(1)
            correct_preds += preds.eq(targets).sum().item()
            total_samples += targets.size(0)
    epoch_test_loss = running_loss / total_samples
    epoch_test_acc = 100. * correct_preds / total_samples

    scheduler.step()

    history['train_loss'].append(epoch_train_loss)
    history['train_acc'].append(epoch_train_acc)
    history['test_loss'].append(epoch_test_loss)
    history['test_acc'].append(epoch_test_acc)

    print(f"Epoch {epoch:02d}/{NUM_EPOCHS}   "
          f"Train: loss={epoch_train_loss:.4f}, acc={epoch_train_acc:.2f}%   "
          f"Test:  loss={epoch_test_loss:.4f}, acc={epoch_test_acc:.2f}%")

    # Store the best model
    if epoch_test_acc > best_acc:
        best_acc = epoch_test_acc
        os.makedirs("models", exist_ok=True)
        torch.save(model.state_dict(), "models/best_efficientnet_b0.pth")
\end{lstlisting}
\subsection*{6. Training and Evaluation Loop}
The model undergoes training for 40 epochs. In every epoch:
\begin{itemize}
  \item The model is trained and then evaluated.
  \item Loss and accuracy are calculated for both sets.
  \item The model's state is saved if test accuracy improves.
  \item The learning rate is updated by the cosine annealing scheduler.
\end{itemize}

Performance statistics are logged after each epoch:
\textsf{Epoch X: Train loss/acc ‚Äî Test loss/acc}

\subsection*{7. Final Outputs}
Upon completion of training:
\begin{itemize}
  \item The best-performing checkpoint is stored at \textsf{models/best\_efficientnet\_b0.pth}.
  \item Performance graphs are generated and saved:
    \begin{itemize}
      \item \textsf{loss\_curve\_efficientnet.png}
      \item \textsf{accuracy\_curve\_efficientnet.png}
    \end{itemize}
  \item The total duration of the training process is reported.
\end{itemize}


\section{Model Architecture}
\begin{itemize}
    \item Base Model: EfficientNet-B0 (with ImageNet pretraining)
    \item Total Parameters: ~5.3M
    \item Classifier Head: Final FC layer to 200 class outputs
\end{itemize}

\section{Training Details}
\begin{itemize}
    \item Loss: Cross-Entropy with 0.1 label smoothing
    \item Optimizer: AdamW (lr=1e-4, wd=1e-4)
    \item LR Scheduler: CosineAnnealingWarmRestarts ($T_0$=10, $T_{mult}$=2, $\eta_{min}$=1e-5)
    \item Configuration: Batch size 64; 40 Epochs; on an NVIDIA T4 GPU
\end{itemize}

\section{Results and Analysis}
\subsection{Performance Metrics}
\begin{itemize}
    \item Top-1 Test Accuracy: \textbf{83.07\%}
    \item Total Training Time: \textbf{43 min}
    \item Model File Size: \textbf{~16.6 MB}
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Training Curves}
\begin{figure}[H]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.75\textwidth]{loss_curve_efficientnet.png}
    \caption{Loss vs. Epoch}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[H]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.75\textwidth]{accuracy_curve_efficientnet.png}
    \caption{Accuracy vs. Epoch}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Observations}
\begin{itemize}
    \item Cropping to bounding boxes markedly enhanced model focus and accuracy by isolating the bird and minimizing background clutter.
    \item Label smoothing and AutoAugment acted as effective regularizers, preventing overfitting and improving the model's generalization by creating diverse training examples.
    \item The Cosine Annealing with Warm Restarts schedule helped stabilize training in later epochs by resetting the learning rate, enabling the optimizer to find better solutions.
    \item \textbf{Curve Analysis}: As seen in Figure 3, both training and test loss curves show a sharp drop early on, signifying rapid learning. The curves then converge steadily, with the test loss closely following the training loss, indicating good stability and minimal overfitting. Similarly, Figure 4 reveals a swift climb in both training and test accuracy, which then levels off. This suggests the model quickly grasped the main features before refining its understanding of finer details. The tight clustering of the train and test accuracy curves points to strong generalization.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Comparison with Related Work}
An 83.07% Top-1 Test Accuracy is a competitive result for an EfficientNet-B0 model on the demanding CUB-200-2011 dataset. While state-of-the-art results on this benchmark often use larger models or more intricate methods (like attention or knowledge distillation), our method provides a compelling balance between high performance and the efficiency of the EfficientNet-B0 architecture. This outcome underscores the efficacy of our chosen preprocessing and training strategies for fine-grained classification.

\subsection{Error Analysis}
For future improvement, a thorough error analysis would be valuable. This would involve inspecting misclassified images to find recurring issues. For example, errors might cluster between very similar species (e.g., different warblers), or result from unusual poses, poor image quality, or difficult lighting. This analysis could inform more targeted augmentation techniques or architectural changes to correct specific weaknesses.

\section{Conclusion}
Our EfficientNet-B0 implementation strikes a balance between performance and efficiency, achieving a competitive accuracy. Future work could explore larger EfficientNet models, semi-supervised techniques, or model ensembling.
